### Y2k Death March Begins.

- **From:** redsky@ibm.net (Thane Hubbell)
- **Date:** 1998-10-13T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.software.year-2000
- **Message-ID:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-UiqsgviyDDoT@Dwight_Miller.iix.com>`

```

I just got off the phone with an associate in the data center in South
Carolina - last week he was newly tapped for the Y2K team.  I tried to
get in touch with him yesterday for some things, and was unable to 
reach him.  He called this morning.  He was at home, but would be 
coming in.  He was home to shower and rest a bit - he had spent the 
last 48 hours at work - doing Y2K remediation.  Says he's too old for 
this.
```

```
I hope your pal is billing by the hour and isn't working overtime for 
free, "because we're all professionals here." If he's working along side
Big-5 consultants, DMR, EDS, IGS, etc, they could be billing $200+ an 
hour while he's working for his $65K/year plus benefits.

..and to you denial-heads, especially the new bunch with the smart 
mouths who think you have a big brain and know it all, ...the reason for
the "Deathmarch" is that the work hasn't been done.  Meetings, 
schedules, a plan, wishing real-real hard isn't the same as cranking
code.

Until the code is fixed and tested, and verified by an independent 
specialist team, you have nothing.

cory hamasaki 444 days, 10,665 hours.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.software.year-2000
- **Message-ID:** `<70io58$5sg$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`

```

> In the words of Major Havoc, an arcade game of Days Gone By... GET
> OUT!!!
>
> DD
>

Major Havoc!!  I really miss Major Havoc.  Finding the shortcuts around level
7 was my greatest achievement in grad school.  Anyone know where I can find
him on a CD-ROM??

I'm gonna join Bill Lynch -- we all need a 12-step program.  "Hi, I'm Lori,
and I'm powerless over y2k."

The 12 Steps of Y2K Anonymous:

1.  Admitted we were powerless over y2k.

2.  Came to realize that only a Power greater than Bill Gates or even the U.S.
General Accounting Office could manage y2k.

3.  Turned our lives and our will over to that Power as we understood it.

4.  As that Power directed us, began buying rice and beans.

5.  Soon moved to buying tools and seeds.

6.  Next we looked at plans for harnessing solar power and buying generators.

7.  Finding generators too expensive, we thanked Providence for the
wood-burning stove that came with the living room and the large supply of
cheap paperbacks that somehow survived the last move, despite our vows to "go
through everything and only pack the things I REALLY use."

8.  At amazon.com we found survival manuals and gardening lore.

9.  On the Internet we began to reach out to like-minded individuals, finding
a support group and sometimes some great flame wars.

10. We graduated from teetering on the edge of doubt to full-fledged paranoia.

11. We considered the purchase of small arms.  Some of us went on to make the
purchase.

12. Having had a spiritual awakening as a result of our preparations, further
researches, and extended state of anxiety, we attempted to carry the message
of Y2K Preparation to others and to continue preparing in every aspect of our
lives.


Lori S.
from The Bunker in Bensalem, PA

-----------== Posted via Deja News, The Discussion Network ==----------
http://www.dejanews.com/       Search, Read, Discuss, or Start Your Own
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-10-13T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<700r20$sik$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-UiqsgviyDDoT@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <7kepWhCNP4qd-pn2-WdYId8GVdjsZ@localhost> <3623c1d1.5022616@news.sgi.net>`

```

Lane Core Jr. wrote in message <3623c1d1.5022616@news.sgi.net>...

>But "focussing" does it. That's the buzzword I've seen in the past couple
>of weeks: "Our focus is on getting this done..." or whatever.


Here's a story for you.

Once upon a time, three brothers went out west to make
their fortune.  They started three farms, one raising beef,
one raising poultry, and one raising swine.  And make their
fortune they did.  Several years later, they were ready to
incorporate, and were at a loss for what to call the new joint
farm.

They decided to wire back home to Mom, and ask her, so
that they did.  They received back a single word telegraph. It
said "Focus".

Now they could not figure that out at all.  I mean, why on earth
would they call their farm that?  So they wired back to Mom one
more time.  "Why focus?".  In return they received another one
word telegraph.  "Websters".

Scratching their heads in bewilderment, they finally flashed on
they idea of looking up "focus" in Webster's dictionary.

There, in black and white, was their answer:

Focus: Where the sun's rays meet.
```

```
In article <700qhn$sd4$1@news.igs.net>,
   "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com> wrote:
+
+cory hamasaki wrote in message <7kepWhCNP4qd-pn2-WdYId8GVdjsZ@localhost>...
+
+>Until the code is fixed and tested, and verified by an independent
+>specialist team, you have nothing.
+
+
+I would not let God test my code.  I'll test it myself, thank you.
+;<)
+
In general, the programmer who wrote the code is one of the last people
who should be testing it. This is due to at least two factors: first,
programmers generally devise test cases that serve to confirm the
hypothesis that the program works (i.e. it does what it's supposed to)
but we tend not to generate test cases that explore whether our programs
do *not* do that which they are not supposed to do. Second, the author
of a program *by definition* will not generate test cases that explore
the behavior of the program under unanticipated conditions -- any
condition that the author did not anticipate wasn't coded for! The best
testers are those who have only a vague idea of what the program is
supposed to do or not do. In one of my previous jobs, we had a clerk
named Pat who was an excellent tester, despite being a little "slow."
Pat somehow managed to do everything wrong, even when you'd swear there
was no way to make a mistake, and she could crash any program she
touched. The Holy Grail of our development efforts was a "Pat-proof"
program. We never made it.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Geek perseverance vrs the Sorokin Effect?

- **From:** "Sharon McGhee" <smcghee@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 1998-10-13T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.software.year-2000
- **Message-ID:** `<01bdf6fe$5e08c100$c64b460c@default>`
- **References:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-UiqsgviyDDoT@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <700d4q$nsu$1@inetserv.arrow.com> <fKQU1.1085$Mc5.1575@news13.ispnews.com>`

```
Shouldn't we be even a little worried about the Sorokin Effect?

Tim Burke <asciiset@hotmail.com> wrote in article 
> 
> Don't worry, all you Pollyannas out there.  Gary North's "Great Geek
> Migration" will NEVER HAPPEN!  All is well with the world!  The geeks and
> the super-geeks who keep your civilization running, who keep the lights
on,
> who make sure those 1.1Billion stock transactions are processed every day
on
> Wall Street -- who keep the mainframes running that control the railroads
> which transport your food -- they will all stay faithfully at their
desks,
> hammering away at code 24-7 for the next 15 months, with LITTLE PERSONAL
> GAIN TO SHOW FOR IT ALL -- so that you can keep living in your
technolgical
> paradise.
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Tim Burke

The title of Russian sociologist Pitirim Sorokin's book says it all:
*Man and Society in Calamity: Effects of War, Revolution, Famine,
Pestilence upon Human Mind, Behavior, Social Organization and Cultural
Life.* (1942, reprinted 1968)

As a student during the Soviet Revolution of 1918-1922, Sorokin knows first
hand of the massive social disruptions caused by revolution and famine.
Disturbances of emotional life, total cognitive focus on the calamity,
increased mental instability, etc., are among the various phenomena he
describes. Chap 2 looks at "How Calamites Affect Our Cognitive Processes,
Desires and Volitions." A calamity "manifests itself in an increasing
incapacity to concentrate on objects unrelated to the calamity, ... in a
decreasing autonomy and self-regulation of our thoughts, independently of
external stimuli; ... In brief, calamities promote the growth of mental
disorderliness and disorganization." p. 35

"This does not mean that the entire population exhibits these
effects ... The proposition means simply that the disorganization
of mental life will be accentuated by any serious catastrophe. ...
The general reasons for such effects are rather evident. Adequate and
fruitful thought demands a considerable degree of detachment from the
disturbing influences of external forces; otherwise one cannot concentrate
on a problem and think it through."

This, then, is the Sorokin Effect: large-scale upsets make focused thinking
difficult, or even impossible. Programmers no longer can concentrate on
fixing their computer code, engineers find their thoughts filled with
concerns about their families and disaster preparations, making it harder
to locate and repair embedded systems. There WILL be a marked decrease or
contraction in the work capacity of individuals, especially as the
catastrophic magnitude of Y2K becomes clearer. What used to take 100
manhours to accomplish will suddenly take twice as long, maybe even longer.
Y2K budgets will balloon, totally out of control. Again, it will be the
Sorokin Effect at work.

"Calamities create a situation in which powerful external stimuli
constnaly intrude upon the field of consciousness - both mental
and emotional - and deprive the mind of the requisite autonomy.
...[becomes] incapable of thinking coherently, intensely, and
creatively ... " "During the famine years of 1918 - 1922 in 
Russia most of the professors and intellectual workers complained of a
decline in their intellectual energy and capacity. Similarly, concentration
and accuracy fell off in many occupations, such as those of printers,
bookkeepers, and clerks." p. 39 Could we add to this list: computer
programmers? or
technicians monitoring nuclear reactors? Any others?

So, during the Y2K meltdown, just when the problem solving 
ability of the technological professionals will be in greatest 
demand, greatest need, it will be on the decline. This will be
known as the Sorokin Effect.
Glen
```

```
(Crosspost to software.year-2000 removed.)

These are the kinds of posts made by people who have no clue about
how the real world functions.  They can only imagine the negative
possibilities, and have absolutely no concept what measures can be
taken by people to avoid or get around them, even when computers do
fail.  Let me give an example.  Say the U.S. welfare check printing
system fails.  All it would take to 'get by' is for programmers to
write a kludge which would print the previous months checks again.
Yes, it would be a mess, but it would cover 95% if the task.  When
the ox is in the ditch, people rally around to get it out.  Do not
underestimate the capacity of millions of intelligent, motivated,
resourceful people to make things work in an emergency.

If Germany and England could keep their economies going during the
unimaginable devastation during WWII, when the very infrastructures
were virtually annihilated, people will survive Y2K.  Civilization
has faced and overcome difficulties in the past which make the worst
possible consequences of Y2K trivial by comparison.  The Soviet
Union lost over *20 million* people, but their society did not
collapse.  And civilization is going to collapse if telephones fail
or electrical power is off, or banks are closed for a few days?  Ha!

Not that anyone cares, but how bad do I think Y2K will be?  Only God
knows for sure, but I do not believe it will be catastrophic.  And I
certainly don't think it will bring down civilization.  If we were
not working on the problem, it *would* be catastrophic.  But we are
working on it, and the vast majority of critical systems will be
fixed.  The societies most dependent on computers are also the ones
most able to correct the problems.

Jerry Peacock wrote:
>
>And here's a prediction from a computer user regarding 'people to do the
>actual work.'
>1. There are not enough people or pens in Manhattan to record the
>transactions on just the NY Stock Exchange.

So, how many people are going to starve because N.Y. Stockbrokers can't
do a trade?  The same number that starve to death when a snowstorm shuts
Wall Street down for a few days: *none*.

>There are not enough people in the STATE of New York to manually process
>just the checks that stream through the Federal Reserve Clearing House
>in one day.

Any bank which is not Y2K compliant will be shut down by the Feds months
before 1/1/2000.  They have already made this clear.  Do you think banks
are going to run the risk of being shut down for months by the Feds?  Ha!

>2. If the computer switches in the telephone system don't work, can we
>really go back to "Hello Central. Your party please?"

And how many people are going to starve to death because they cannot
make a telephone call? *none*

>3. Have you considered the computers that mix the ingredients in
>prescription drugs or your water filtration plant? Or that monitor the
>pasteurization temperature for your canned goods?

And how many food or drug processing companies have no employees who
can read a thermometer or adjust a machine? *none*

>4. How about the old "Semaphore bug?" Computers can't operate without
>electricity and the power company can't operate without computers.

And how many power companies have no engineers who can override a
computer controlled switch, or run a busbar around one? *none*

>5. During the inconvenience,' all manner of do-badders will take the
>opportunity to wreck havoc: From foreign religious fanatics fomenting
>terrorism to your local crack-head who wants - well, who knows what a
>crack-head wants.

And how many policemen or firearms will be unable to function because
it turns 1/1/2000? *none*
```

```
On Thu, 15 Oct 1998 03:12:09 GMT, Judson McClendon <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
wrote:
>>3. Have you considered the computers that mix the ingredients in prescription
>>drugs or your water filtration plant? Or that monitor the pasteurization
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>controlled switch, or run a busbar around one? *none*

Reading comp.risks might shake your faith in this. There are numerous stories
of problems caused by systems where no provision for a manual override was made
(Too expensive! The computer's always right! Putting everything on the computer
allows us to hire cheaper staffers) and/or the personnel aren't trained and
have no idea what to do. Yes, it is unbelievably stupid to trust the computer
like that and criminally stupid not to train personnel to handle the things
that should never happen but do. No, this hasn't prevented people from doing
it.

However, I don't agree with the c.s.y2k posters who are waiting for TEOTWAWKI.
I'm planning on it being as bad as a fair-sized earthquake; it will take time
to disable the computers, bring in skilled technicians, etc and this will all
be happening at the same time, so there could be a couple weeks of disruption.
I live in California, so it's a precaution I'd want to take any way, just in
case.

-- The opinions above are my own and may or may not match those of my employer.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...

>These are the kinds of posts made by people who have no clue about

I am with you on that.  In fact, what most people are forgetting
is that 99% of all Y2 problems only affect transactions that
SPAN the year end.  Transactions that start in the new century
should have no more problems than the ones that started
after 1900.  There will be bank managers pissed off at customers
that cannot produce an accurate ageing list in Jan, 2000, but since
they discount everything more than 90 days old, that sort of
thing will self correct within 90 days, even if we do nothing.

I have one customer that does not use credit.  After talking it over,
they decided to just balance forward everything to Jan 1, 2000
as their "century end".  I wrote the programs in a day, and tested
them the next.  They know that some reports will be unreliable
for one month.  So what?
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
>(Crosspost to software.year-2000 removed.)
>
…[52 more quoted lines elided]…
>make a telephone call? *none*


How about elderly people who depend on their phone for contact to the
outside world?

I would like to point out that around here one suburb city just experience a
complete failure of its phone system, one side of the central telephone
switch was shut of by technicians for repairs, the redundant side did not
take over.  For 26 hours an entire city about 50,000 was without phones.
You would not believe the extremes police had to go to make sure they could
get peoples reports, the problems faced by the city hospitals and clinics,
they used cell phones to handle life and death situations.  The police set
up reporting centers all over the city where people could come and report
crimes.  I know a number of people there, and they were all very angry.
No tradgedies occured.  But if it had gone on for very long, there would
have been severe problems.
What did the phone company do?  They flew in a replica switch, a solution
that would not work if the problem was a Y2K issue.

If the communications of the entire metropolitan area failed, there would be
extreme problems.  Lets just take my company, for example.  I work for a
grocery chain.  If all our systems survived Y2K, and the phone lines died,
the stores couldn't send us orders, we couldn't send orders to the
warehouses, the warehouses couldn't ship to the stores or order from the
vendors, and we certainly couldn't send the orders to the vendors, either.
If even a small part of this communications network goes down, our company
goes into mild convulsions, warehouse overtime costs run $10,000 per hour of
delay per warehouse.  I've seen this go on as long as 16 hours, and by then
things were already desperate.
If this problem took only 24 hours to solve, it would have a major impact.
If it took 48 hours to solve, you would begin to see shortages on the
shelves of almost 200 stores.
I can only imagine what would happen to really advanced supply chain
operators, ones with tight just-in-time systems in place.

>>3. Have you considered the computers that mix the ingredients in
>>prescription drugs or your water filtration plant? Or that monitor the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>can read a thermometer or adjust a machine? *none*


How many people could manually adjust their car's engine to run without the
electronic feul inginition system computer running properly?  *None*.  (That
is not to say that fuel systems will fail, only that human intervention
would be pointless.)

>>4. How about the old "Semaphore bug?" Computers can't operate without
>>electricity and the power company can't operate without computers.
>
>And how many power companies have no engineers who can override a
>computer controlled switch, or run a busbar around one? *none*


I wonder how sensitive these systems are to small adjustments.  Especially a
system that would, say, require many adjustements per second.  How many
humans can do this? *none*

>>5. During the inconvenience,' all manner of do-badders will take the
>>opportunity to wreck havoc: From foreign religious fanatics fomenting
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>it turns 1/1/2000? *none*


How many people can have their identities checked on a Y2K broken police
computer?  License plate checks?  Crime Lab equipment with embedded systems?


Will it all fail?  No.  Will some of it fail?  Probably.

If the post office can't get its vending machines ready for the new 20s
before the fact with full foreknowledge of the problem and an easy fix
available, I doubt that much else in government will be ready.


You're right, we don't know what will happen.  <shivers>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Chris Osborne" <chris_n_osborne@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 1998-10-16T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36275927.0@news2.uswest.net>`
- **References:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-UiqsgviyDDoT@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <700d4q$nsu$1@inetserv.arrow.com> <fKQU1.1085$Mc5.1575@news13.ispnews.com> <3623F52C.718F466E@mindspring.com> <362551e0.0@news.intnet.net> <dKdV1.1234$9R2.1618673@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <36260de3.0@news3.uswest.net> <706ngs$rld$1@news.igs.net>`

```

Donald Tees wrote in message <706ngs$rld$1@news.igs.net>...
>Chris Osborne wrote in message <36260de3.0@news3.uswest.net>...
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>lie to a computer when it boots,  I will send them a letter telling them


>>>>>>and nobody figures out how to lie to lie to a computer<<<<<<

I'm not sure what you meant here . . .  Even if the second "lie to" is just
a typo-repeat, the sentence still doesn't make any sense.  Unless you're
talking about "putting the year back", as you mention below.

>how.
>Sure Y2 is a pain in the ass, but you doomsdayers are either just plain
>stupid, or trying like hell to make a fast buck.


Donald,

I'm trying to make a fast buck?
Of course since I'm not making a fast buck, I must be stupid.


>There are dozens of ways to make things work after the year 2000.
>The laws of physics will not change.  It may cost people lots of money,
>but you can be absolutely assured that if critical things start to fail,
and
>lives are in danger, then we can just put the date back a year.


Put the date back a year?  How do you do that to an embedded chip with the
software burned into it?
Lets say that most of the telephone switches have remotely updatable flash
memory (they don't, of course).  Now, we put the date back a year, and they
immediately fail to talk correctly to those switches that either are Y2K
compliant, or those switches that weren't fixed and just rolled over to year
"00".  I'm not saying that communication would fail, but you'd get all sorts
of inaccurate recording of call transactions.  Some switches think its 2000,
some 00, and some 99.
And that's just the telephone switches.
Since everyone is not going to evenly implement a date-back solution, then
everyone who does will be in a completely incompatible situation with
everyone who doesn't.  I can't imagine what would happen under such
conditions.

As the days roll on, how do you tell the difference between transactions in
1999 and those in 2000 (since the date has been put back, all transactions
in 2000 appear as if they happened in 1999).  Sure they're in separate files
so over the long haul you'll be able to date-alter the appropriate files,
but when online systems that cover more than one year interact with these
files, they won't be able to tell the difference.

Lets say %90-100 of our banks are fully Y2K compliant on time.  Since Japan
is doing practically nothing, lets say they do the "put the date back" idea.
One of our banks and one of their banks try to send each other money.  One
side is going to lose out here.
Would anyone working in banking here care to speculate as to what would
really happen when, say Bank America tries to send Japanese Bank X a billion
dollars at 0800 Jan 1, 2000 when the Bank America transaction reads the
current time and the Japanese Bank X records its time at 0800 Jan 1, 1999?
Remember, Jan 1, 2000 is a Saturday, and Jan 1, 1999 is a Friday.


>>How many people could manually adjust their car's engine to run without
the
>>electronic feul inginition system computer running properly?  *None*.
>(That
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I wasn't saying car systems would fail, as I clearly pointed out, I was
using them to illustrate that some systems require a degree of control that
cannot be supplied by manual intervention.




Oh, and for the record, I don't recall mentioning the end of the world,
doomsday, or any other Armageddon style disaster.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net>
- **Date:** 1998-10-17T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36289487.4BE3@swbell.net>`
- **References:** `<36275927.0@news2.uswest.net> <3298289.14958.16581@kcbbs.gen.nz>`

```
Richard Plinston wrote:
> 
> In message <<36275927.0@news2.uswest.net>> "Chris Osborne" <chris_n_osborne@yahoo.com> writes:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> on complete cluelessness.

I am not exactly an expert on telephone switches, but I can assure you
that they care about date and time, if only for billing purposes.  They
record the date, time, and duration of your calls and forward this
information to the billing systems (which typically run on huge
mainframes).

For some kinds of service, calls may be routed differently according
to time of day or day of week.

If the switches aren't Y2K compliant, my guess is that most calls
will still go through, but the telephone bills will be massively
confused in ways which are difficult to predict.

In practice most telephone companies are scrambling to fix all the Y2K
problems they can find.  The switches run on software provided by the
switch vendors, who are presumably scrambling as well.  Naturally 
there can be no guarantee that all the bugs will be fixed, but most
of them will.  The remaining bugs, if any, may indeed disrupt service 
-- that's happened before, unrelated to Y2K, and will no doubt happen 
again.

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/cobol.html
Opinions expressed are my own, and are not necessarily those of
my employer.
```

```
In message <<36289487.4BE3@swbell.net>> "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net> writes:
> > 
> > First please establish that 'telephone switches' know of or care
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> mainframes).

That depends on what is being referred to as 'telephone switches'.
There certainly needs to be some parts of the system that
care about date and time for recording and billing purposes,
but many  'switches' are simple electronic relays that 
receive signals and switch lines.

> 
> For some kinds of service, calls may be routed differently according
> to time of day or day of week.

And this not be done by the switches themselves but by the
exchanges where the routing information and switch
signalling is generated.

> 
> If the switches aren't Y2K compliant, my guess is that most calls
> will still go through, but the telephone bills will be massively
> confused in ways which are difficult to predict.

If the _billing_ system needs to deal with variations in
year representation then it is possible that it can do
this.  It _could_ receive a date of 00 and know this was
not 1900, it _could_ receive a date of 80 and know that
this is not 1980 or 2080.

It was proposed that there would be no way to reset dates on
various components of a system.  If that were true then
the dates could never be set in the first place.

Your ramblings are simple ill-informed fantasy hype that
does nothing to help the real problems that may occur.

For example if the phone system worked by sending a
signal to the 'billing system' at the start of a call
and then sent one at the end of that call the only
requirement for year 2000 issues would be in the 
billing system itself.  There would be no need for
the switches to know of or care about the date and 
time because the time would be set by the billing system.

So first it is important to become informed about _how_
the system works, _then_ to predict where the problems
may arise.

Making up fantasy stories on issues that you are clueless
about does not alert people to any real issues.
```

```
Your telephone is connected by a cable pair to what I have called a
"switch," also known as "central office" or sometimes "end office".
I believe you have referred to it below as an "exchange."  In the
U.S., at least, a switch is most assuredly *not* a simple electronic
relay, possibly with rare exceptions that haven't been upgraded for
decades.  It is a large specialized computer, typically running a
specialized variant of Unix.

Further levels of switches may get involved when the calling and
receiving phones are not attached to the same central office.

It is true, however, that for some kinds of specialized services
the switch delegates the decision-making to some other box.  How and
when this happens varies from one telephone company to the next.

actually February 29, the solution would not be so simple.

My point was that the billing is probably more vulnerable than the

easier for a mainframe to handle, and there is no need for real-time
operation.
```

```
> specialized variant of Unix.

In which case it is accessible and can be subjected to

The point that I was raising is that switch need not necessarily
know or care about the date or time. In the case of logging 
calls for billing it only needs to send a 'starts now' signal
and an 'ends now' signal for each call.  The logger can put
its own date and time information on.

In fact with different time zones and other problems associated
with keeping exact time it is best to not rely on what
various items think the time is but to have some centralised
time keeper.

The companies have obviously solved such problems as dealing
with summer time changes.  We are variously 11, 12  or 13

dates didn't agree.  I can ring LA now and it is 4pm Sunday
while in LA it is midnight Saturday, the phone system won't
stop working because of this.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net>
- **Date:** 1998-10-18T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3629F4B6.1C13@swbell.net>`
- **References:** `<36291B34.183@swbell.net> <3298290.11944.19327@kcbbs.gen.nz>`

```
Richard Plinston wrote:
> 
> In message <<36291B34.183@swbell.net>> "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net> writes:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> tests and can be corrected.

Yes, the software can be tested and fixed, specifically by the switch
vendors.  Since the software is sensitive to dates, the switches *must*
be made Y2K-compliant, if they aren't already.

> > Perhaps by "switch" you are referring to customer-premise equipment
> > such a PBX.  Otherwise I don't understand your distinction between
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> original poster.

For the record, your statement was:

	And this not be done by the switches themselves but by the
	exchanges where the routing information and switch
	signalling is generated.

...clearly indicating that switches and exchanges were not the same
thing.  It was this distinction that I did not understand.

> > The mainframe billing system could indeed apply windowing to a
> > two-digit year.  But if the switch thought it was March 1 when it was
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> its own date and time information on.

You can dream up all kinds of ways that the system *might* work, but
the telephone industry must deal with the way it *does* work, and it 
does not work as you describe.

Nor can the way it works be readily changed.  There are a multitude
of telephone companies, constantly exchanging messages about calls
according to industry-wide protocols and conventions.  Even the
simplest change to the message format requires months of negotiation
beforehand, and considerable tricky and delicate coordination at
cutover.

> In fact with different time zones and other problems associated
> with keeping exact time it is best to not rely on what
> various items think the time is but to have some centralised
> time keeper.

Perhaps, but the time zone where the billing system resides is not
relevant.  When date and time matter, whether for call routing or for
billing, what matters is the date and time at the switch.  It is not 
obvious to me that the various switches need to be precisely 
synchronized, but if they do, there are ways to synchronize them
without involving the billing system.

> In any case if it sends information in the form of a time_t
> as seconds since 1980 it won't matter whether it calls it
> Feb 29 or March 1, it is still the same number.

The use of a time_t seems neither likely nor wise:

Unlikely because it is foreign to the billing systems' environment.
In all the mainframe systems I have ever worked on, I have never seen
a timestamp of this type -- the date was always Gregorian or Julian,
and the time, if present, was tacked on to the end.

(I am assuming here that the billing system is on an MVS-type 
mainframe, which is typical in the U.S., but I don't know about the 
rest of the world.)

Unwise because a time_t has no well-defined portable meaning.  It is
a concept from the C language, which allows each implementation to 
define it however it pleases, specifying only that it is an arithmetic
type capable of representing times.  It need not represent seconds,
nor honor any specific starting date.  It needn't even be an integer.
For that matter the usual use of a 32-bit integer will run out of 
seconds sometime around the year 2037.  A good old 8-digit Gregorian 
date, or a 7-digit Julian, will have a much longer shelf life.

I'm not sure what we're arguing about any more, but I'll try a
summary.  All my remarks apply to the U.S., and most especially to
the major phone companies such as GTE and the former Bell companies.

1. Do telephone switches care about dates and times?  Yes.  In an
alternative world they might not have to, but in our world, they do.

2. Do telephone switches need to be Y2K-compliant?  Yes.  At the very
least we cannot merely assume otherwise.  The switch vendors and
telephone companies must take the issue seriously.

3. Will the switches be ready on time?  Almost certainly.  It's
difficult to be sure that any large complex system is 100% bug free,
even for a restricted category of bug.  There may be a few bugs left,
but I expect the impact to be localized, minor, and considerably short
of apocalyptic.

In the unlikely event of a Y2K disaster, it won't be because the 
telephone industry didn't work hard to avoid it.  If there is no
Y2K disaster, it won't be because the telephone industry didn't have 
to do anything about it.

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/cobol.html
Opinions expressed are my own, and are not necessarily those of my
employer.
```

```
There are all sorts of ways to set the date of an embedded chip.  The
trouble comes not in how it is set, reset, or otherwise controlled, but in
the fact that there are only two digits allowed for the year.  A two digit
year telco switch will probably go right on working, but will bill all sorts
of incorrect dates.  When the printers start printing incorrect bills
(assuming the billing programs don't choke), it's doubtful that any telco
company will actually ship the ridiculous output.  The problem comes from
how long it will take to fix each and every problem with software patches.
And the real problem will not be fixed until the switch is replaced.


>If they do not do it by magic then there must be some mechanism
>(if there really is one at all) to set the date and time when
>the machine is first powered.  Thus there _is_ a mechanism to
>reset it.


See above.


>> Lets say that most of the telephone switches have remotely updatable

>> memory (they don't, of course).  Now, we put the date back a year, and

How amusing.

Please establish to me why automated metal foundry computer control systems
would "know or care" about the date.  You would think that computer mindless
going about their job of moving giant buckets of molten metal around to pour
into molds would not care about the date at all.  They did though, and when
they ran into a slight problem with the number of days in the year due to
being improperly programed, they all simultaneously quit and cost the owning
company $1,000,000 in ruined equipment because the operators who took over
for the frozen control systems couldn't handle the temperatures well enough.
I didn't bring the book along to site company and city name, but I'll do so
tomorrow.

Now, a telco company switch receives and routes your call and immediately
records the date and time for the telephone company, if it didn't the
telepone company would be unable to bill you.  Several switches can be
involved in routing a call, and every one of them records the date and time.
It is obvious why this is a problem if one of the switches involved records
the year in only two digits.

How many switches are affected by this?  Will they all still be two-digit
bound by the deadline.  I don't know.  I certainly didn't say (go back and
read what I said).  The telepone companies and switch manufacturers won't
say, either.


>
>[rest of message deleted as being more mindless ill-informed
>rubbish]
>

Doubly amusing.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** riplin@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)

```
In message <<362a6ad9.0@news3.uswest.net>> "Chris Osborne" <chris_n_osborne@yahoo.com> writes:

> >How did the 'embedded chip' get the date set in the first place ?
> >How did it keep ticking away between manufacture and being
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> the fact that there are only two digits allowed for the year.  A two digit

That it has a '2 digit year' is simply an assertion of yours.
There is nothing inherent in a 'chip' that enforces a
particular year format.

> year telco switch will probably go right on working, but will bill all sorts
> of incorrect dates.  When the printers start printing incorrect bills
> (assuming the billing programs don't choke), it's doubtful that any telco
> company will actually ship the ridiculous output.  The problem comes from
> how long it will take to fix each and every problem with software patches.

If they haven't already been 'fixed' and tested.

> And the real problem will not be fixed until the switch is replaced.

Simple minded fantasy based on zero information.

> >> And that's just the telephone switches.
> >
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> tomorrow.

It was Comalco in Bluff right here in NZ.  This had nothing to
do with 'embedded chips' but was entirely in the mainframe
control computer.  The scheduling program had its database
of work required indexed by day number and it didn't get to
day 366 at the end of a leap year.

This has zero to do with 'embedded chips' or 'two digit years',
the cranes and pots and other foundry equipment neither knew
of or cared about the date or time.  These operated by simply
being told what to do by the centralised mainframe regardless
of when they were told.  When the mainframe failed to locate
the days schedule (due to a _programming_ error) it did not
issue any control commands.

This provides no evidence foryour fantasies on 'embedded chips'
or 'switches knowing the date', it has no relevence to the
issues you attempted to raise.

> 
> Now, a telco company switch receives and routes your call and immediately
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> the year in only two digits.

Why does 'everyone of them record the date and time' ?  In my
world it is only necessary for the exchange I connect to to
record the start of my call, the number I call and the end
of the call.  All billing information could be worked out
from that.  It may be that every exchange is recording all
calls that arrive for their own local use, such as analysis
of traffic and recharging, but the idea that every 'switch'
sends its own bill to me is flawed.

Given that I can call places where the time is up to 25 hours
different from my time then your 'idea' that all 'switches' 
must have the same date and time is simply wrong.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Chris Osborne" <chris_n_osborne@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 1998-10-15T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36260903.0@news3.uswest.net>`
- **References:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-UiqsgviyDDoT@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <700d4q$nsu$1@inetserv.arrow.com> <fKQU1.1085$Mc5.1575@news13.ispnews.com> <3623F52C.718F466E@mindspring.com> <362551e0.0@news.intnet.net> <3625890B.9E585B71@att.net>`

```
Back on December 31st, 1996, at the stroke of midnight, a New Zealand<sp>
metal foundry's equipment ground to a halt.  Management and operators leapt
up to take manual control of everything.  None of the computer controlled
systems could be made to work, and no one could figure out why.

Two hours and two time zones later, in Tasmania<sp>, at another metal
foundry (the one that invented the control system used by both companies),
exactly the same thing happened at midnight.

Cause?  The programmer who had created the system had forgotten that 1996
was a leap year.  When Jan 1, 1997 rolled in, the computers couldn't handle
that fact that the previous year had 366 days instead of the expected 365.

They fixed it inside 24 hours.  The first company lost over a million
dollars in permanently damaged equipment.


I expect most flaws to be like this one.  However, it is _where_ they occur
that matter.

For instance, there are over 80 nuclear power plants in the US.  Each and
every one of them is a custom job.  There might be a common component here
and there among them (if they ordered from the same company), but I doubt
there are many.  Their software, both embedded and non-embedded, was written
by different people in different places who were not cooperating.
I don't really know what will happen here, but the history of extreme
irresponsibility of the AEC and the NRC does not inspire me.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

- **From:** "Alan Maitland" <AMaitland@NBCI.Net>
- **Date:** 1998-10-13T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.software.year-2000
- **Message-ID:** `<7019qu$46i$1@news-2.csn.net>`
- **References:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-UiqsgviyDDoT@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <700d4q$nsu$1@inetserv.arrow.com>`

```
Manzari,

Manzari wrote in message <700d4q$nsu$1@inetserv.arrow.com>...
--snip--
>schedule.  Each meeting results in the usual pep talk about putting our
>noses to the grindstone and putting first things first and getting our
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>project.  If this is treason, then make the most of it.

I think that your priorities are most correct.  You and your family must
always come first.

It has been said that if you can't take care of yourself, how are you going
be able to take care of others?  I think that understanding this concept is
vital.  How does an employee work effectively if they are mentally
preoccupied with a stressful personal issue.  Studies in this area that I
have read tend to suggest that they don't.  Worse yet, those under extreme
personal stress tend to become more susceptible to sickness over time.

There is a rather nasty business philosophy that considers employees purely
as resources.  As one might try to tap as much oil out of an oil well in a
reasonably efficient and cost effective way, managers are trained to extract
as much labor from employee resources as is possible to do in a cost
effective way.  They, in turn, are measured on those results.

Such, I think, is a rather Machiavellian management style that contributes
to early burn out and retardation of efficiencies after a point.  I don't
personally subscribe to it.

I believe that if you treat people fairly and understand their priorities,
you will encourage a natural loyalty and willingness to go above and beyond
if called for during the more difficult times.  Obviously, not all will
succeed in such an environment.  Those who do not, tend to be weeded out
fairly quickly.

A 15 month death march is not reasonable by any measure for anyone whether
married or single.  No matter how kind the company is to you.

Companies that choose such a course will find that they will be rewarded
with many internal difficulties.

It is rare for a programmer to just jump in and begin coding something.
Programming is not like driving a car.  You can't just jump in an fire up
the motor and drive off.

A programmer needs to understand local programming conventions (non-geek
translation: the style and rules by which a given company codes its
programs, you might think of it as being similar to the different ways that
a reporter could tell the same story) and the mix of applications any
company uses, which changes from company to company.

Hardware, operating systems and the like have subtle and not so subtle
differences between the different manufacturers.  Even from revision to
revision of operating systems, things can change.  Because of a new product
or set of system services, the way things may are done may change.

Bottom line, lose a trained resource and no matter who you replace them
with, there will be some ramp up time for the replacement.  This is time
luxury that companies do not have where it comes to Y2K remediation.

Those managers who feel that flogging the oar slaves for months on end will
make the ship go faster are likely going to end up sending their companies
adrift.

Best,

Alan Maitland
President
NBCI.Net

http://2000.Experience.Net/
Pay attention - stay alive
```

```
critical high tech projects. Designing a chip, for example.

I'm not saying the practice will fix the Y2K problem, just that "death
marches" are a fact of life in places like the Silicon Valley.
```

```
Just in the middle of a simple Y2K test.  (we do not calculate dates
just report 'em).  The things that trip us were surprising:

company.  Whoops didn't check that one!

2.  Running in a MVS region and having problems therefore restoring the
MVS region back to scratch again.  Whoops your tape management list just
disappeared and you overwrote those backup tapes that you made.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** pitchl@tdbank.ca (Lew Pitcher)
- **Date:** 1998-10-20T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<362c7af8.85223003@news21.bellglobal.com>`
- **References:** `<70g28j$4ue$1@news.igs.net> <19981019192106.27116.00002964@ng63.aol.com>`

```
On 19 Oct 1998 23:21:06 GMT, fboll@aol.com (FBoll) wrote:

>>>There is a reason it's called a "DEATH MARCH".>>
>>>It's when companies, force EXISTING staff to take on NEW responsibility (for
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>thing was outsourced. 

Nope. Lots of companies have a permenant, full time programming staff. Outsourcing
isn't always the way to go (proprietary information or code, heritage code, etc.)

>If they did have EXISTING staff the whole Y2K thing would be a non-issue, just
>another product release like any other change. 

Sure :)  

Since when have you known a "user community" to pay for a product release which
adds no new functionality, and fixes no existing problems? New releases are used
when your corporate user has asked for, and is willing to pay for changes to the
application, not when the corporate IS staff wants to "fix things". "Bug fixes" are
authorized only when the bug bites and causes problems in existing production.

The Y2K issue is slightly different; it's being treated like a government mandated
change (think withholding tax changes, etc.); something that the corporate user
doesn't want, but has to be done anyway. It's not a non-issue because some businesses
are taking the position that the cure will cost more than the problem.



Lew Pitcher
System Consultant, Delivery Systems Architecture
Toronto Dominion Bank

(pitchl@tdbank.ca)


(Opinions expressed are my own, not my employer's.)
```

```
Lew Pitcher wrote in message <362c7af8.85223003@news21.bellglobal.com>...

>The Y2K issue is slightly different; it's being treated like a government
mandated
>change (think withholding tax changes, etc.); something that the corporate

Now *that* is an interesting statement.  I have had the feeling for quite
a while now that the biggest Y2 problem is probably the governments
and tax collection.  As Judson has said, things like welfare can be solved
easily ... they are monthly runs, and in a pinch, the previous month's
run could be re-run.  Audits and yearly tax records are quite a different
story. There is also the fact that problems would be brought to light by
complaints. (As is true of most business systems).

On the other hand, there are very few people that would complain
if they received an assessment that said their income tax was not
due for one hundred years.  They might even decide to wait it out
and see what happened.
;<)
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
>Donald Tees wrote:
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>violate tax laws than they do current drug laws? :-)


The same reason that gangsters got out of liquor when prohibition
ended.  Criminals can seldom compete with competence.  It is the
illegality that keeps the price so high that people with no skills can
make fortunes.  Do you really think that the average drug
dealer/pimp/thug could run a multinational liquor company efficiently?

Cigarettes and liquor have proven that over and over.  Tax them
so heavily that there are huge profits in smuggling, and you create
a huge business for criminals.  That in turn finances more crime.

Notice as well that the numbers game has died out as a criminal activity
as all the states have got into the lottery game.  Legalised gambling has
killed off 90% of criminal gambling.  It cannot compete, and nobody
wants to deal with thugs.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT - Gov't drug policy (was: Y2k Death March Begins.)

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** William Lynch <wblynch@att.net>
- **Date:** 1998-10-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<363010E6.FBB9B876@att.net>`
- **References:** `<70g28j$4ue$1@news.igs.net> <19981019192106.27116.00002964@ng63.aol.com> <362c7af8.85223003@news21.bellglobal.com> <70i0a4$jqm$1@news.igs.net> <RB%W1.425$Se2.633786@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70i336$lc8$1@news.igs.net> <slrn72sfao.di.cadams@cadams-ntw.acucorp.com> <362ECD69.4BBB971A@att.net> <70n8qh$240$1@news.igs.net> <UkIX1.1026$Se2.1886209@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70nqeo$et9$1@news.igs.net> <boLX1.1980$xx4.1981762@news3.mia.bellsouth.net> <362FEDD2.EC9ECE8E@mindspring.com>`

```
SkidMike wrote:
> 
> again, i intrude on a thread.  folks, forget the tax on drugs garbage.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> revenue so bad that we should make moral what is evil.

IMHO, the current "War on Drugs" is a direct parallel with Prohibition,
with (predictably) the same result. There are lots of issues here, and
I'm certainly don't believe I know them all - a couple, off the top:

1. when a substance is legalized we expect the price to go down (vis a
vis the drug gang's price, even with a lot of tax on it) and the purity
to go up & to be reliable;

2. as someone else pointed out, the drug gangs are not long on brains &
running multi-national companies, plus they'll bail when they can't
obtain their 1000% (or more) profit;

3. re: gun usage & violence, look thru some history books from the 20's
& 30's (USA, only) - *lots* of gun use & violence there (ever heard of
the Valentine's Day Massacre?). Al Capone wasn't elected criminal boss
of Chicago, anymore than John Gotti was elected to head the Gambino
family by its board of directors;

4. it's not clear that drug usage would rise after the end of
Prohibition - the USA didn't become a nation of alcoholics when the
Volstead Act was repealed. The thrill of getting the illegal substance
is gone, but it's really easy to get. No gov't policy is going to
eliminate all addiction, whether to nicotine, alcohol, cocaine, heroin,
chocolate, etc. You have to let people make these decisions for
themselves, i.e., to smoke or not, and understand that *some* will
become addicted.

5. I think "evil" is more a religious decision, or attribute, rather
than a political (or societal) one. Keep in mind that MJ and cocaine
were legal in the USA (elsewhere, too?) until the 30's. I see it as a
trade off that was made in some smoke-filled back room: "Okay, okay,
we'll give you the booze; but we have to get something in return." "Pot,
cocaine? Deal" (Interesting aside, the field of psychiatry was developed
by someone with a mother fixation and a cocaine habit <g>)

6. I assume most of us have seen the ads for a drug-free America, from
the Partnership for a Drug Free America. It is my understanding that
this partnership is funded by the tobacco & alcohol lobbies, which is
why "drug free" doesn't include tobacco or alcohol.

Okay, off my soapbox. I'm certainly interested in hearing what others
have to say,
Bill Lynch
```

```
In article <362FEDD2.EC9ECE8E@mindspring.com>,
   SkidMike <skidmike@mindspring.com> wrote:
+again, i intrude on a thread.  folks, forget the tax on drugs garbage. 
+do you really want to make crack or heroin a legal taxed drug?  look
+what the "crack ho's" and "junkies" do for a hit now.  if the price goes
+up due to a tax, there's just more stealing and whoreing to cover it. 

Mike, you're missing the point. The high price of street drugs is due
principally to their illegality: there is risk of prosecution and
incarceration inherent in their distribution, thus the price rises to
compensate for the risk. If street drugs were legalized, the prices
would plummet. They would still be substantially cheaper than current
illegal drugs even if taxed at 100%. 

+don't make the vicious circle bigger.  better to rid the culprit one
+victim at a time than make the culprit legitimate.  we don't need gov't
+revenue so bad that we should make moral what is evil.
```

```
docdwarf@clark.net wrote:
>
>Do we need a government to tell us, via laws, what is moral *or* evil?

Tsk! Tsk!  DD, I would have expected you of all people not to make
such a totally unfounded statement! :-)  You know as well as I that
*all* laws have a moral base, either directly or indirectly.  Laws
are one of the primary means a society uses to establish and support
its moral base.  This is why laws tend to be more strict in moral
societies and more lax or not enforced in decadent, immoral societies.
For example, you should well know that homosexuality was a capital
offense in early, moralistic Greece, as well as the early U.S.  But
in later, decadent years of decay and collapse, homosexuality was
freely and publicly practiced in Greece, as we see it happening in the
U.S. today.  Similar trends can be seen in the Roman Empire and other
well studied societies.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

_(reply depth: 22)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-10-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Q33Y1.1380$Se2.2550873@news4.mia.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<70g28j$4ue$1@news.igs.net> <362FEDD2.EC9ECE8E@mindspring.com> <70q1dg$b56$1@clarknet.clark.net> <NH0Y1.1357$Se2.2476023@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70q7fm$hh4$1@clarknet.clark.net> <RB1Y1.1364$Se2.2502614@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70qbn1$aut$1@news.igs.net>`

```
Donald Tees wrote:
>
>Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>road or the law to drive on the left?

The *fact* that there is a law is moral based: that order and safety
is moral, and that disorder and chaos is imoral.  That it is immoral
for the government to allow, through inaction, people to be killed
in automobile accidents because of disorder.  Nobody said that every
practical detail of all law was based on a specific moral principle,
but that the basis of all law is moral, directly or indirectly.

>Most laws are based on precedent, in fact.  In the United States,
>they are based on the constitution.  Good thing, too, as we all
>have different morals.

You are looking only at the surface.  Why would we have the law in
the first place?  Where did the precedent come from?  Previous laws,
based on moral grounds.  And why would precedent be an issue?  For
reasons of fairness and order; moral reasons.  The very fact that we
have laws is a moral issue, and the laws we have are based on morals.
Morals differ between societies, but all laws are based on morals,
either directly or indirectly.  All law says that you must or must
not do certain things.  The implication is that it is 'right' (moral)
that this conduct be enforced.  Any other basis for law would be
astonishingly foolish.

>I take it from the comment above that you retract your remarks
>on the laws governing the IRS? Or is that different?


As a society, we believe it is 'right' (moral) to have a government.
The government must have funds to operate, therefore we have laws
to collect revenues by taxes and other means.  Therefore, that we
have tax laws is based on a moral issue.  How the revenue is
collected is also a moral issue: which method is fairest, etc.  As
imperfect humans, our laws are not necessarily just or fair or moral.
But the *basis* of all laws is a moral one.  My personal belief is
that it would be better to tax spending than income.  This is a moral
and practical judgment.  The specific details of a given law may be
refined by practical rather than moral reasons, or both.  But the
reason for the law to exist, any law, is a moral one.

Since you say our laws are based on the Constitution, you may find it
interesting that a 10 year university study was done (I can get the
details, if you like) on the documents produced during the writing of
the Constitution.  One of the items researched was which authorities
were quoted, and how frequently.  The most frequent authority quoted,
more than twice as frequently as the second most frequent authority,
was the Bible.  If laws, particularly the Constitution, are not based
on moral issues, this is most peculiar, don't you think? :-)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

_(reply depth: 21)_

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-10-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<70sgs2$b7s$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<70g28j$4ue$1@news.igs.net> <362FEDD2.EC9ECE8E@mindspring.com> <70q1dg$b56$1@clarknet.clark.net> <NH0Y1.1357$Se2.2476023@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70q7fm$hh4$1@clarknet.clark.net> <RB1Y1.1364$Se2.2502614@news4.mia.bellsouth.net>`

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...

>We either have laws based on morals (which all laws are) or we have
>anarchy because we have no laws.  Which do *you* think we need? ;-)


If those were the only two possibilities, then we would indeed
be in a lot of trouble.

It is interesting that the debate we had over Cobol
standards VS Cobol style seems to exactly parallel
our current argument over civil law and public morality.

Your stance, in both cases is that "good" style and
"standard" should be/are synominous.  My stance, in
both cases, is that the two are quite different things;
that style/morality *must* be a matter of individual
choice for evolution to work, while law/standard is
simply the minimal rules required to make the system
work.

One of the more interesting points is that I do not
really think our aims are any different ... in the
case of Cobol, the best code.  In the case of social
systems, the most rewarding living conditions for all
individuals.

I believe that Law, like any set of programming rules, is
often faced with the situation where there is no "neat"
solution.  We are forced to pass laws that have nothing
to do with morality simply to make the system work. My
point regarding driving on either the left or the right
side of the road is indicative ... the real point is
not "which" side of the road, but rather that we all
have to agree on one side or the other in order to make
the system work.  If there is a moral prerogative involved,
then it has to do with regard for the rule, not the rule
itself.  It is not driving on a particular side of the
road that is "immoral", but driving against the traffic
and killing people as a result. One has to look at the
results to evaluate a rule; it cannot be taken as an end,
only a means.

The problem arises when the world changes, and the rule
no longer works. There are cases, like the year 2000
problem, where the rule actually causes the problem. Then
we have no choice ... we have to change the rule. If we
have the philosophy that standards/laws/rules/morality
are exactly equivalent, then we are crippled. If we take
the stance that they overlap, and have similar qualities
but different purposes, then we can adapt.

In closing, I will say that all my training, and all my
experience, leads me to the conclusion that absolutes and
"written in stone" conclusions ultimately fail.  The
development of individual judgement is the essence of
both professional and social evolution, not the acceptance
of judgement by others.

As I said in an earlier post, good programming is based
on philosophy as is anything else.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Y2k Death March Begins.

_(reply depth: 22)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-10-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dO4Z1.88$5Z2.218576@news4.mia.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<70g28j$4ue$1@news.igs.net> <362FEDD2.EC9ECE8E@mindspring.com> <70q1dg$b56$1@clarknet.clark.net> <NH0Y1.1357$Se2.2476023@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70q7fm$hh4$1@clarknet.clark.net> <RB1Y1.1364$Se2.2502614@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70sgs2$b7s$1@news.igs.net>`

```
Donald Tees wrote:
>Judson McClendon wrote:
>>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>be in a lot of trouble.

Perhaps I mis-communicated there.  I believe you would agree that
to advocate no law whatsoever is tantamount to advocating anarchy.
My contention is that the very fact and foundation of any system of
law is a moral statement, to wit: that it is the correct and proper
thing to do, a moral position.  Since all law is founded on this
moral basis, then theoretically you have a binary choice: some law
(which is a moral statement) or no law, which is anarchy, but also
a moral statement.

>I believe that Law, like any set of programming rules, is
>often faced with the situation where there is no "neat"
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>itself.

I agree, but would add the fact that a law, any law, or even
that there should be law, is a moral statement, even if the
specific goal of a given law might not be framed as a moral
issue.

>In closing, I will say that all my training, and all my
>experience, leads me to the conclusion that absolutes and
>"written in stone" conclusions ultimately fail.

Here we greatly differ, because of our different world views.
As a believer in Christianity and God's authority, I do believe
that God's laws are absolute.  Since you do not agree with that
worldview, you do not see it the same way.  I suspect a person
would need to believe in some absolute authority such as God to
believe in absolute laws.
```

_(reply depth: 20)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-10-25T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<eoGY1.3588$xx4.3454496@news3.mia.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<70g28j$4ue$1@news.igs.net> <19981019192106.27116.00002964@ng63.aol.com> <362c7af8.85223003@news21.bellglobal.com> <70i0a4$jqm$1@news.igs.net> <RB%W1.425$Se2.633786@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70i336$lc8$1@news.igs.net> <slrn72sfao.di.cadams@cadams-ntw.acucorp.com> <362ECD69.4BBB971A@att.net> <70n8qh$240$1@news.igs.net> <UkIX1.1026$Se2.1886209@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70nqeo$et9$1@news.igs.net> <boLX1.1980$xx4.1981762@news3.mia.bellsouth.net> <362FEDD2.EC9ECE8E@mindspring.com> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-3vwFlPB1JFSL@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <6Q4Y1.825$Kp5.4918523@news1.atlantic.net> <36317DFE.D423ECC2@att.net>`

```
William Lynch wrote:
>
>Sorry, disagree here. IMHO gov't should protect others from being harmed
>by me, and vice versa. Let me worry about myself. I believe I should
>have the right to self-mutilate (ear piercing, tattoos, etc.) to the
>point of death.

I have refrained from making any comments one way or another on this issue.
I will say that philosophically, I agree with Bill's statements above.  But
the problem with many drugs, including alcohol, is that common use creates
situations in which innocents are eventually certainly going to be put in
danger or abused, because the drugs inhibit or distort the normal self
restraints necessary to living in a civilized society.  There are millions
upon millions of children and spouses being beaten, tortured and abused
because of alcohol or other drug use in adults.  If it were simply a case
of an adult making an independent decision to whack out on drugs, and other
innocent people would not be directly harmed, the moral issue would be much
clearer.  Properly founded, government has two primary functions.  One is
to defend the country from outside aggression, and the other is to protect
its innocent citizens from harm by others.  When the widespread availability
and use of a substance inevitably causes mass suffering and harm among
millions of innocent citizens, what is the moral authority for government to
abrogate its primary function?  Do we think that addicts, once able to obtain
their drugs legally, will then be respective of the rights of others?  Do
alcoholics who buy alcohol legally refrain from abusing their children or
spouse because the alcohol was legal?  When it comes to rights, is it not
broad precedent that you restrict an individual's rights at the point when
exercise of those rights begins to harm the innocent?  At what point after
a person begins to ingest or inject a drug should the proscription be made?
Before of after the person is incapable of evaluating the proscription and
subsequent consequences?

I want to make one more comment about the moral basis of law.  As I have
said before, the fundamental basis of all law, indeed the concept that we
even have law, is a moral basis.  The shared belief that it is 'right' to
have a set of rules to protect the innocent is a moral belief.  When a new
law is formulated, the underlying base concept is that there is a 'right'
(moral) thing to do, and it is 'right' that government have and exercise
the authority to enforce it.  The details and specifics of the law may have
many practical aspects, but the fundamental issue is to do and enforce what
is 'right' (moral).  Because of this, it is an inherent attribute of law
that laws are one of the ways the morality of a society is articulated and
enforced.  It has always been so, and will always be so.  It is impossible
that law exist and not be so, in principle.  However, law like all things
created by imperfect humans, laws are not perfect.  The law does not
necessarily express accurately the true morality, and certainly never the
complete morality, of a society.  Some moral issues are suitable for
framing into law, and some are not.  And in a broad society, law cannot
express the morality of all citizens, but at best a general consensus of
those moral issues thought properly expressed as law.  But laws have never
been, nor will laws ever be, the primary or best way for a society to
establish knowledge of, respect for, and continuity of, that society's
moral base.  These things must primarily be done through the family, church
and school.  It is foolish to say that you cannot or should not "legislate
morality", for that is in fact what law is.  But it is true that law alone
is not sufficient.  Morality is established in a people through common
belief in moral principles.  Some of these common beliefs are articulated
into laws which protect against gross violations of moral principles, which
would fall under government mandate (see above).  In a generally moral
society, laws will suffice for this.  But law was never intended, and will
never achieve, turning an immoral people into a moral people.  With the
widespread rejection of any absolute basis (i.e. God, the Bible) for
training our young citizens in the moral rules of conduct, our society has
departed from the moral base on which it was founded.  This is why many
people feel that many of our laws are 'old fashioned' and out of date.  When
you cut your anchor chain, you are going to drift.  The question for us as a
society, and as individuals, is "Are we drifting into safe water, or into
dangerous water?"  Even casual perusal of statistics for all aspects of
violence, abuse and drug use, in fact of negative societal indicators across
the board, for the many years leading up to and following, the O'Hare
Supreme Court ruling on prayer in public schools, is enlightening. I suggest
that anybody who is interested simply take a good look at the statistics,
the dates, and consider causality.
```

```
> Properly founded, government has two primary functions.  One is
> to defend the country from outside aggression, and the other is to protect
> its innocent citizens from harm by others.  

Almost always, function #1 is a subset of function #2.  Where it isn't
(a country is being "liberated"), it is not a valid function.

The function of a government should be to protect individuals from the
powerful.

However, this usually conflicts with the real function of the governors,
which is "to make a real impact".   Politicians are like the rest of us
- they want to be relevant.  (We want our code to be used, we love to
mentor others, etc.).  That means passing laws and "fixing" things.

It also allows for being a dictator and being able to line up people and
have them shot.  Fortunately, we are in a stage of human development
where economics favors giving the small guy power.  A politician who
gives the small guy economic power and freedom will himself have more
wealth and power than the dictator in the country next door.

Often countries have dictators who get replaced by new dictators.  Even
if the revolution was created for the "people".  Power goes to the
people permanently only when such power is useful to enhance power for
the powerful.
```

```
actually, howard, it has been shown time and again by the courts that
the government (i.e. the police) have a pretty limited obligation to
protect individuals.  our system of laws from the constitution on down
is more punitive than pro-active.  the founding fathers IMHO intended
for each citizen to provide for their own personal safety and security.
basically, the gov't's function is protection only after the offender
has been apprehended.  unfortunately, that is the great loss of our
society since the protection (as in imprisonment or death) is far too
crippled by the court systems.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
>Rick Smith wrote in message ...
>>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Morality has nothing to do with it.

Consider laws prohibiting the overthrow of government.

A dictator overthrows the government but is not prosecuted.

The dictator passes a law prohibiting the overthrow of the government.

The dictator's government is overthrown no one is prosecuted.

If it were morally wrong to overthrow a government, some would
have to be prosecuted for the insurrection.

Laws against treason and insurrection are amoral. These laws
are passed because they are needed to protect and preserve the
government. Such laws have no moral basis, whatsoever! It is neither
right nor wrong to overthrow a government; yet, each government
must have such laws to prosecute those who attempt and fail.

[However, it is always wrong to overthrow *my* government. :-)]

Simply put, some laws do have a basis in the morality of those
who created the laws. Some laws are amoral. Hence, it is not
true that *all* laws have a moral basis.
```

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-10-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<712sfp$q88$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<70g28j$4ue$1@news.igs.net> <19981019192106.27116.00002964@ng63.aol.com> <362c7af8.85223003@news21.bellglobal.com> <70i0a4$jqm$1@news.igs.net> <RB%W1.425$Se2.633786@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70i336$lc8$1@news.igs.net> <slrn72sfao.di.cadams@cadams-ntw.acucorp.com> <362ECD69.4BBB971A@att.net> <70n8qh$240$1@news.igs.net> <UkIX1.1026$Se2.1886209@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <70nqeo$et9$1@news.igs.net> <boLX1.1980$xx4.1981762@news3.mia.bellsouth.net> <362FEDD2.EC9ECE8E@mindspring.com> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-3vwFlPB1JFSL@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <6Q4Y1.825$Kp5.4918523@news1.atlantic.net> <36317DFE.D423ECC2@att.net> <eoGY1.3588$xx4.3454496@news3.mia.bellsouth.net> <Pp2Z1.19$Ak.79@news1.atlantic.net> <sB2Z1.50$5Z2.150323@news4.mia.bellsouth.net> <XK5Z1.79$Ak.53@news1.atlantic.net>`

```
Rick Smith wrote in message ...

>Simply put, some laws do have a basis in the morality of those
>who created the laws. Some laws are amoral. Hence, it is not
>true that *all* laws have a moral basis.


I asked my lawyer what he thought of this supposition (I play
pool with him), and he was MOST emphatic ... NO!

As he put it, "We like to think that a few laws are morality based,
but most have nothing to do with morality.  They are like the rules
of chess, you need rules to play the game.  The actual rules are
not important; what is important is that everybody understands
them and is playing by the same rules. Then you can both play
the game.  That is why precedent is so important to law. The
purpose of court is NOT to discover what is good or bad, but to
discover what the rules say about the case, and to provide
consistency".

"That is why laymen get their shorts in a knot (sic) over what
they see as injustice by the courts; they are thinking in terms
of right and wrong in a moral sense. It is the courts job to see
that every case gets treated consistently, not fairly. Fair is
defined by the law, not the other way around".
```

```

Donald Tees wrote in message <712sfp$q88$1@news.igs.net>...
>Rick Smith wrote in message ...
>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>
"... but most have nothing to do with morality. ..." sounds like
what I said. This implies that some laws have something to
do with morality.

Consider laws banning public nudity, drunkness, etc. The
presence and wording of the laws and the severity of the
punishment will varying among communities based upon the
offense to the sensibilities (or morality) of the citizens of that
community.

Public nudity, in and of itself, is neither moral nor immoral; but
a community may pass a law based upon the consideration
that public nudity is immoral. Hence, the basis for that law is
morality.
-------------------------------
Rick Smith
e-mail: < ricksmith@aiservices.com >
```

```
Rick Smith wrote in message

>Public nudity, in and of itself, is neither moral nor immoral; but
>a community may pass a law based upon the consideration
>that public nudity is immoral. Hence, the basis for that law is
>morality.


Well, I think there is overlap.  According to my dictionary, morality
is concerned with "good and evil".  I think that there is a fair amount
on consensus on basics ... not many would condone murder for
example as "good". (Unless there is a war, of course, in which
case it becomes illegal not to murder for the state).

I look at the bulk of law though (about 5% of law is criminal) as being
closer to programming standards.  Consistency is the most crucial
aspect for the simple reason that within a given group of programmers
it is important to be able to understand each other's code.  A degree
of conformity is required for that.  Similarly, we all have to understand
things like ownership in a community, or we simply cannot function
together.  The concepts of ownership and property are very well defined
by law as a result.

As to whether they are "good" or "evil" ... that is a sociological/cultural
value.  I do not see it as an intrinsic value at all, but simply as an
aspect
of our society that either works or does not.
```

```
Donald Tees wrote:
>
>I look at the bulk of law though (about 5% of law is criminal) as being
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>aspect of our society that either works or does not.

You are confusing the details of 'laws' with the foundation of 'law'.
When a culture establishes a government and gives that government the
power to create and enforce some system of law, it is done because the
people believe it is the 'right thing' to do.  Would you argue that
they do it because they think it is the 'wrong' thing to do? :-)  Any
time a judgment is made that it is right and appropriate to restrict
people's activity to a certain standard, that judgment is a moral
judgment.  The specific details and form of the particular laws is
irrelevant.  The individuals doing it may have good or evil intent, it
does not matter.  The the absolute foundation on which any government
establishes law is a moral basis.  Whether at any time the laws created
could be judged as 'moral' or 'immoral' by any standard is irrelevant.
Whether the individual laws have a practical or good/evil purpose is
also irrelevant.  No society, no culture, could or would construct a
legal system which was not thought 'correct', 'right' or 'appropriate'.
All of these are moral assessments.  The very concept that something
could be 'right' or 'wrong', in any broad context, is a morals concept.
```

```
Donald Tees wrote:
> 
> Rick Smith wrote in message ...
> 
(snip)
> "That is why laymen get their shorts in a knot (sic) over what
> they see as injustice by the courts; they are thinking in terms
> of right and wrong in a moral sense. It is the courts job to see
> that every case gets treated consistently, not fairly. Fair is
> defined by the law, not the other way around".

Yes, if the two parties agree to stipulate that 1 + 1 = 3, then 1 + 1 =
3 (for that case, anyway). It has, unfortunately, nothing to do with the
real world. I think we're seeing a major problem inherent in our current
system, in which the judges are lawyers who made good. A long time ago
(in a galaxy far, far away) judges were respected lay people who
listened to the lawyers' arguments and points of law, then rendered a
judgment. These tended to be more real world and "fairer" decisions.

Bill Lynch
```

```
Donald Tees wrote:
>
[quote from lawyer]
>"That is why laymen get their shorts in a knot (sic) over what
>they see as injustice by the courts; they are thinking in terms
>of right and wrong in a moral sense. It is the courts job to see
>that every case gets treated consistently, not fairly. Fair is
>defined by the law, not the other way around".

Laymen "get their shorts in a knot" because they instinctively know
what the lawyers have been trained to forget.  That the fundamental
reason why people create and submit to government in the first place
is because it is intended for the public 'good'.  When this purpose
becomes subordinated to some abstract concept not based on 'doing
good' you get tyranny and injustice.  This merely illustrates more
clearly what I have been saying all along.  The foundation of law is
a moral one.  Law is created for the good of people, not the other
way around.
```

```
> >Sorry, disagree here. IMHO gov't should protect others from being harmed

Counterpoint:  What impacts you also impacts your family and others. 
That was the argument made to proscribe gambling (until the state
discovered how much it could make from it), and even drugs.  Currently
the state will sometimes fight to take custody of a child who is denied

limits for different people.

choices).  Governments will force people to evacuate in the face of
natural disasters.  Governments can build dams and levies to "punish" a
river for flooding.
```
